  created: '2012-10-30 01:56:38'
- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "There is \"free\" as in you don't need to compensate the designer, then there's
    what people call \"libre\" which means free for redistribution. Technically all
    four possible combinations of those two can exist, although in practice you can't
    really charge money for a libre font because within minutes somebody will put
    out an identical free version. Of course it can get pretty complicated pretty
    quickly, for example if you believe that \"open-source\" means that the tools
    to make the original also need to be free (or even libre).\r\n\r\nThe fatal flaw
    in the \"things must be libre\" philosophy is simple: freedom is over-rated. It's
    nice, but it's just another thing that needs to be factored in life. Anybody who's
    <em>really</em> concerned with social responsibility knows very well that big
    chunks of individual freedom need to be sacrificed for the greater good. Don't
    believe all this Western hype.\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"
  created: '2012-10-30 04:58:39'

- author:
    name: quadibloc
    picture: 118515
  body: "hrant:\r\n<cite>The fatal flaw in the \"things must be libre\" philosophy
    is simple: freedom is over-rated. It's nice, but it's just another thing that
    needs to be factored in life.</cite>\r\n\r\nSurvival is the most important thing
    in life, because it is the true <em>sine qua non</em>: and if you are not free
    (by one important definition) your survival is at risk.\r\n\r\nThis is why having
    a free press and free elections are vital; without control over your local government,
    you can be enslaved or slaughtered at whim.\r\n\r\n<cite>Anybody who's <strong>really</strong>
    concerned with social responsibility knows very well that big chunks of individual
    freedom need to be sacrificed for the greater good.</cite>\r\n\r\nThis, though,
    I don't disagree with, in <em>some</em> senses and contexts. Without governments
    with the power to tax and conscript, the Western democracies would have been conquered
    long ago by Stalin or Hitler. So I reject Libertarianism and similar ideologies.\r\n\r\n<cite>Don't
    believe all this Western hype.</cite>\r\n\r\nBut here I strongly disagree. I think
    the Western vision of liberty, handed down in civics courses, is basically accurate.\r\nIt
    does contain a few things that are flawed, though.\r\n\r\nThere are self-serving
    rationalizations that define the stealing of land from indigenous people as something
    other than theft. because they didn't \"develop\" the land by practising agriculture.
    To take away the hunting grounds people need to feed themselves is as murderous
    as denying someone air by smothering him with a pillow (which also puts paid to
    the \"property is theft\" nonsense on the other side).\r\n\r\nThe effort to combat
    demagoguery, which is understandable, fails to address the justification for the
    welfare state, if not socialism; children are not at fault for being born into
    poverty. But the other side can retort that the responsible are not to blame for
    the feckless.\r\n\r\nMy logical conclusion is that someone does owe you a living,
    but it is your parents, not the State; and thus the State may legitimately demand
    financial responsibility as a qualification for reproduction. How it can do that
    without outraging the Right (interfering in intimate affairs) or the Left (effective
    genocide against disadvantaged minorities) is... left as an exercise for the reader."
  created: '2012-10-30 07:51:09'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "<blockquote>It just occurred to me, what\u2019s the difference between an
    open source font, and a free font?</blockquote>\r\n\r\nLicensing and intent. Saying
    that a font is <em>free</em> or <em>open source</em> does not really provide sufficient
    information about how it can be used. The kind of open source license(s) a font
    is released under can have an impact on other software the font is used or embedded
    with and this issue alone is the reason that a font exception clause that can
    be applied to the GNU GPL exists:\r\n\r\nhttp://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-faq.html#FontException\r\n\r\nhttp://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPL_font_exception\r\n\r\nhttp://www.fsf.org/blogs/licensing/20050425novalis\r\n\r\nTo
    the best of my knowledge the [[http://scripts.sil.org/cms/scripts/page.php?site_id=nrsi&id=OFL|Summer
    Institute of Linguistics (SIL) Open Font License]] is the only open source license
    specifically formulated for use with fonts. Others, such as the [[http://www.apache.org/licenses/|Apache]],
    [[http://opensource.org/licenses/MIT|MIT]] and [[http://creativecommons.org/licenses/|Creative
    Commons commune of licenses]] are either not specific enough about whether fonts
    subject to them make related content open source by association or very specifically
    require attribution.\r\n\r\nThe good thing about the above licenses is that they
    are very well documented and discussed, thus potential users have no excuse for
    not understanding what liberties and restrictions they impose upon fonts released
    under them.\r\n\r\nFonts labelled as <em>free</em> can usually be separated into
    two categories: 1) fonts released under a standard commercial license but free
    of charge, either directly to customers or bundled with a larger purchase; 2)
    fonts released with a basic, limited or no license and free of charge. In the
    second case it is often unclear what restrictions, if any, are imposed on the
    fonts and as such their usage potential is often extremely limited particularly
    for commercial usage.\r\n\r\nAnother major factor in deciding to use or abuse
    any of the above is quality or distinct lack thereof and the work involved, for
    example, in modifying an open source font to work optimally may end up costing
    more in man hours and time spent testing and revising than it would have cost
    to license a commercial font that was already there."
  created: '2012-10-30 09:43:15'

    <em>libre</em>."

    along the way.\r\n\r\nDealing with what stands in the way of that, especially
    for other people, is a loftier goal, but still one that is understandable."

- author:
    name: dishdesigner
    picture: 111471
  body: "I think it's more interesting to consider the meaning of \"open source\"
    here less in the sense of commerce than in the sense of intellectual property
    and potential benefits.\r\n\r\nTaken in the broader context of computing and software
    development, anything that's \"open source\" implies that the code generating
    a product is open for inspection, customization, and extension by end users who
    have myriad needs that the original designer cannot fully accommodate or even
    predict.\r\n\r\nIn terms of font development, that would mean an \"open source\"
    font is posted publicly with something like the GNU license, and all the character
    outlines and font metrics are included in some widely accessible format.\r\n\r\nThis
    raises the possibility of \"crowdsourcing\" font design...(ask yourself in how
    many fonts you've ever wanted to change just a few wordstopping characters to
    make them more usable, right?! Everyone's got a list...)\r\n\r\nIt can only be
    a good, democratizing thing in the long run to prioritize the needs of end users
    over the rather narrow and often self-driven creative pursuits of one type designer,
    no matter how talented or smart. We know from research in many areas that the
    knowledge and creativity of the masses will nearly always outperform that of an
    expert individual.\r\n\r\nBeyond just a list of the open-source fonts that have
    been attempted so far, it would be a great feat of digital social justice to compile
    a statistical list of the fonts people would most like to change, down to individual
    glyphs, prioritize them, and let those fonts loose to the masses to start whittling
    and improving for everyone to benefit.\r\n\r\n(Type geek party game: design one
    glyph and pass it on...)"
  created: '2012-10-31 06:45:50'

    Just that whether something is open or closed source isn't a direct indicator
    of the quality of the solution. "

- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "<blockquote>ask yourself in how many fonts you've ever wanted to change just
    a few wordstopping characters</blockquote>\r\n\r\nFor some weird reason though
    that seems to happen almost exclusively with free fonts...\r\n\r\nAlso, there
    might be a reason something was designed in a certain way that a non-designer
    won't understand... until it's too late. For example if you see a \"g\" that feels
    a bit vertically cramped and you decide to make it drop lower, you're making things
    worse not better, and you won't even know.\r\n\r\n<blockquote>We know from research
    in many areas that the knowledge and creativity of the masses will nearly always
    outperform that of an expert individual.</blockquote>\r\n\r\nI for one know no
    such thing. And it's counter to common sense that you could know how to do something
    without expending the effort to learn how to do it.\r\n\r\nOne area that I think
    laymen can certainly help though is feedback: a mechanism for expressing opinions
    about what's wrong with a font (or what could be added) would be awesome. Some
    designers will be too proud to pay heed to outside opinion, but the way I define
    Designer the <em>good</em> ones will be all ears.\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"

- author:
    name: aluminum
    picture: 110335
  body: "Yes, true. I don't think the concept of open source implies 'anyone and everyone
    are capable of improving upon it'. It merely states that they have the legal right
    to give it a shot if they want. \r\n\r\nAnd that's why the more succesful open
    source software projects have fairly rigorous peer review and gatekeepers. Might
    be impractical at the level of a font, but possible. \r\n\r\nThat said, I think
    the more practical applications of wanting to release a typeface as open source
    is to offer it as a tool for the broader open source software movement. It allows
    open source software access to type designs that otherwise wouldn't make it into
    the products due to licensing issues. "
  created: '2012-11-01 06:09:11'

  children:
  - author:
      name: aluminum
      picture: 110335
    body: "I think that's a theoretical fear rather than a real one.\r\n\r\nI can
      grab a copy of the Apache web server source code, make inept changes, and then
      give it away, but no one is going to use my version over the main branch version.
      Most licenses require that any derivations be uniquely named as such to avoid
      confusion as to which is the 'main' branch. \r\n\r\nNow, granted, that's all
      accepted standard procedure when it comes to open source software projects,
      and maybe that level of project formality hasn't been applied to open source
      typefaces specifically. "

    simplified definition would be a typeface with working files for which the creator
    has granted permission for others to modify and re-distribute. That\u2019s significantly
    different than free.\r\n\r\nAs far as boiler-plate responses, I do so because
    I see the same mistakes over and over. Using acronyms and poor hyperlinks makes
    it harder for the lay-typographer to participate, and creates accessibility issues
    for visually impaired readers.\r\n\r\nAnd foul language just reflects poorly on
    the entire community."

- author:
    name: "T\xE9 Rowan"
    picture: 121227
  body: "To assist documentation:\r\n\r\n\"R/I/B/BI\" &ndash; Regular, Italic, Bold
    and Bold Italic. O for Oblique may replace the I.\r\n\r\n\"SIL Open Font License\"
    &ndash; An open-source (recognised as such by the Open Source Initiative) font
    licence developed by SIL (Summer Institute of Linguistics) for their own use and
    adopted by others.\r\n\r\n\"GPL with font exception\" &ndash; The General Public
    License with an exception that specifically exempts (PDF) documents embedding
    the font(s) from the GPL.\r\n\r\nFree &ndash; The problem with 'free' is that
    there are two kinds of it in English: Free beer (<em>gratis</em>, for free) and
    free speech (<em>libre</em>, freedom).\r\n\r\n- - - - -\r\n\r\n@Renaissance Man
    &ndash; There is still a lot of un- and badly-documented slang and jargon out
    there. Also, your infosheet was badly organised, with many assumptions. Using
    tags would have been more helpful, say:\r\n\r\nTypeface: Andada\r\nFoundry: Huerta
    Tipografica\r\nLicense: OFL 1.1\r\nDesigner: Carolina Giovagnoli\r\nDescription:
    ...\r\nWebfont URI: ...\r\n\r\nGood tagging makes searches easier, too."

- author:
    name: quadibloc
    picture: 118515
  body: "@T\xE9 Rowan:\r\n<cite>URI = Uniform Resource Identifier, the New Order name
    for the humble URL or web link.</cite>\r\n\r\nI was going to say...\r\n\r\nNo!\r\n\r\nThey're
    very similar, and closely related, but they are <strong>not</strong> the same.\r\n\r\nbut
    I see that I was only half right.\r\n\r\nA URL is still what it always was: an
    actual web address, the first part of which has a direct mapping to an IP address,
    its raw or internal form.\r\n\r\nBut there is also what I thought a URI was -
    an URN, or universal resource <em>name</em>; that follows a given site around,
    even as it gets hosted in different locations.\r\n\r\nA URI can be <em>either</em>
    a URL or an URN."

- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "Volkorn by Friedrich Althausen is a nice family of four fonts which was [[http://friedrichalthausen.de/?p=43|originally
    released under a Creative Commons license]] but has subsequently been [[http://friedrichalthausen.de/?page_id=411|updated
    and released under the SIL OFL]].\r\n\r\nThis is a good example of how important
    it is to try and trace the source of a given font to the original designer and
    check the application of a particular license as there are significant differences
    between the freedoms afforded by these two different licenses.\r\n\r\nThe [[http://openfontlibrary.org/en/guidebook|Guidebook
    section of the Open Font Library site]] has a good [[http://openfontlibrary.org/guidebook/supported_licenses|overview
    of and links to information about various open source and free software licenses]]."

    Standard</a> seems to be released under the SIL OFL.\r\n\r\nSo is <a href=\"http://www.scholarsfonts.net/cardofnt.html\">Cardo</a>.\r\n"

  created: '2012-11-06 16:12:45'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "<blockquote>How does 'open source' differ from 'public domain'</blockquote>\r\n\r\nIn
    my experience through the presence of an explicit license. Where I have seen fonts
    \"released\" into the public domain they are accompanied by a statement which
    makes that clear without imposing any kind of license. For example, Barry Schwartz's
    Goudy Bookletter 1911:\r\n\r\nhttp://crudfactory.com/font/show/gb1911\r\n\r\nClicking
    on the \"Public Domain\" link on that page takes you to the Wikipedia entry for
    public domain."
  created: '2012-11-06 16:19:23'

    name: Jens Kutilek
    picture: 113138
  body: "<blockquote>It would perhaps be more accurate to describe them as fonts released
    under an open source license, as much as you might describe fonts released by
    a major foundry as released under a commercial license.</blockquote>\r\n\r\nSorry
    if I wasn\u2019t clear enough, my confusion arose from the terms \xABOpen source
    font\xBB vs. \xABOpen source typeface\xBB, as both were used in this thread, and
    my impression was that they were used to mean different things.\r\n\r\nOpen source
    fonts are easy to define for me, the source being a FontLab VFB file or FontForge
    or UFO file, and being made available together with the fonts under an Open Source
    licence."
  created: '2012-11-06 16:41:21'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "<blockquote>Sorry if I wasn\u2019t clear enough, my confusion arose from
    the terms \xABOpen source font\xBB vs. \xABOpen source typeface\xBB, as both were
    used in this thread, and my impression was that they were used to mean different
    things.</blockquote>\r\n\r\nI am pretty sure that it comes down to them being
    used interchangeably as opposed to referring to different things, at least in
    the context of this thread but detailed re-reading may prove otherwise.\r\n\r\nFor
    my part I have been referring to fonts licensed under a specific open source license,
    as per your definition, sorry if I have added to the confusion by using the terms
    interchangeably. In some cases it may be the intention from the initiation of
    a concept, through the drawing of letters to engineering and distribution to consider
    the typeface as \"open source\" in whatever sense people choose to interpret that
    and the more philosophical debates at the beginning of the thread are certainly
    more along those lines, but as Chris was asking for a list (and as this is something
    I would find useful as well) that is what I have focused on."
  created: '2012-11-06 16:53:55'
- author:
    name: aluminum
    picture: 110335
  body: "Open Source and Public Domain are legal intellectual property right concepts.
    Whether it's type or not is somewhat irrelevant. \r\n\r\nMy (limited) understanding
    of Public Domain is that it can get messy due to how it's defined from jurisdiction
    to jurisdiction. \r\n\r\nOpen Source has one advantage over Public Domain in that
    (ideally) there is a clear license attached.\r\n\r\nSo, an OS product's use is
    dictated by its included license. A PD product's use is dictated by the laws in
    the jurisdiction the product belongs to. \r\n\r\nAt least in the US, if a property
    is public domain, you can take it and do whatever you want with it...give it away,
    resell it, redesign it, whatever.\r\n\r\nMost open source licenses, however, (in
    the context of software) have a clause that any derivative software you make with
    it will need to retain the same open source license (either as a whole, meaning
    your entire product also needs to be open source, or as a noted credit for portion
    for your product, even if your bigger product uses a different license).\r\n\r\nSo,
    with type, I think one distinction could be that with a public domain typeface,
    I could take it, modify it, and sell it. With an open source typeface, I could
    take it, modify it, but would need to redistribute it with the same license as
    the original.  \r\n\r\n(Though the details all come down to the wording of the
    actual license, of course)"
  created: '2012-11-06 17:23:09'
- author:
    name: "T\xE9 Rowan"

  body: "HVB &ndash; The Open Source stuff is still copyrighted, which is why a licence
    is needed.\r\n\r\naltsan &ndash; There's nothing 'seems to' about it. Old Standard
    and the Theano Classical Fonts are, in the author's own words, \"released under
    the SIL Open Font License ...\".\r\n\r\n[[http://googlefontdirectory.googlecode.com/hg/]]
    is the path to the Google Webfont Directory's Mercurial repository. The link 'ofl'
    takes you to the fonts/faces licensed under the SIL OFL. Usually, there are VFB
    and/or SFD files with the TTF/OTF files."

- author:
    name: Mark Simonson
    picture: 110448
  body: 'One little thing: UFO (Unified Font Object) does happen to be RoboFont''s
    native format, but it predates RoboFont by almost a decade and is not exclusive
    to RoboFont. It was created as a non-proprietary format for exchanging and archiving
    font data.'

- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "My bias isn't against the pricing - that would be weak. If somebody makes
    a high-quality product and benefits by giving it away, more power to him*, and
    to hell with the old farts who don't want to adapt to new realities. The problem
    isn't the pricing, the problem is pretending the quality is good enough; the problem
    is dreaming that quality and price are completely detached.\r\n\r\n* I myself
    made very little money from Arasan, but the prestige -I mean within the Armenian
    community- is still going strong after 14 years.\r\n\r\nAnd I totally don't mind
    being ignored by ignorants. Ignoring things -like what actually constitutes quality
    type- is the ignorer's loss. If you can't tell a good font from a bad one -free
    or not- <em>you</em> pay for that first and foremost.\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"

  created: '2012-11-12 02:51:44'
- author:
    name: altsan
    picture: 124930
  body: "As a relative newcomer I can't say I see anything hrant has said here (or
    in any other thread) to qualify as \"ranting and raving\"; indeed, his comments
    generally seem quite fair, if blunt.\r\n\r\nI've put out a couple of open source
    fonts, or attempts at fonts, in my time, and I'd be the first to say that they're
    not up to standard.  (I haven't listed them in this thread for good reason!)  They
    were initially designed for my own purposes, at a time when I was totally blundering
    about blind in terms of design know-how (especially Workplace) - and boy, does
    it show.  I didn't even know what a counter was, let alone understand the first
    thing about proportion and colour.  I've been slowly trying to improve them through
    constant revision, but looking back at earlier versions makes me want to bang
    my head against the wall.\r\n\r\nSuzuran, which has a modest thread going in the
    serif Critique forum, is my first serious attempt to design a font that I wouldn't
    be embarrassed to let real designers see.  I have no idea if I could ever get
    it to a stage where it would be worth a commercial release, but profit isn't my
    motivation at the moment anyway. \r\n\r\nI guess my point is that a commercial
    (i.e. cost-money) font is automatically more likely to be competently designed,
    because if it wasn't it wouldn't be able to sustain itself as a product.  Hobbyists
    designing for fun and education, as I've been, will by default tend to release
    their work for free if at all. "

  created: '2012-11-12 14:14:48'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "<blockquote>Is there a logic to the pattern of some acronyms using a capital
    and lowercase (Lr) , and some using two capital letters (UL)?</blockquote>\r\n\r\nThis
    distinguishes between abbreviations for single word descriptions of weight/style,
    such as Hairline (Hr) and multiple word or part descriptions, such as Ultra Light
    (UL)."

  body: "1. One risks discrepancies when working off-line with other folks' data,
    especially when one should be sleeping.\r\n\r\n2. FontForge defaults to Unix conventions
    for T1 fonts: AFM/PFA or AFM/PFB. AFM-to-PFM conversion utilities exist.\r\n\r\n@Pablo
    &ndash; As much as it sounds like kvetching, much of it was information I needed.
    I found I was two subversions behind on Fabrik, for fanen!\r\n\r\nEdit 1:\r\n\r\nThe
    Fabrik TTFs I have (from a fresh download) have 570\u2013571 kerning pairs, according

  body: "re: PFB+AFM\r\nFonts distributed that way used to include an .INF file containing
    the menu names and style linking. Such distributions could be converted to PFM+PFB
    with the now absent Adobe Type Manager (ATM). \r\n\r\nI know of no utility or
    simple method under current operating systems for the average consumer to do this?
    (I.e., someone without FontLab and technical knowledge of how to copy the .inf
    info into the font manually. \r\n\r\nHmm. I seem to remember a similar conversation
    with Tom 3-4 years ago in the Adobe typography forum \u2026 which is now effectively
    defunct.\r\n\r\n- Herb\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"

- author:
    name: altsan
    picture: 124930
  body: "This is a slight divergence but still I think on-topic (and since I can't
    start new threads this is the best I can do)...\r\n\r\nI've recently downloaded
    and am trying to classify the <a href=\"ftp://ftp.dante.de/tex-archive/support/ghostscript/AFPL/GhostPCL/urwfonts-8.71.tar.bz2\">GhostPCL
    fonts</a> which URW++ donated to Artifex software under the free Aladdin license.
    \ They don't seem to be as widely known or disseminated as the 35 \"core GhostScript\"
    fonts similar donated by URW++, but they provide open source implementations of
    some very nice faces.  Mostly they seem designed to fill in some of the gaps in
    the PostScript v3 core set when taken together with the GhostScript fonts. \r\n\r\nHowever,
    I'm having trouble clearly identifying one or two... and I'm not entirely clear
    on the distinction between some of them.  \r\n\r\nThe name in \"quotes\" is the
    family name stored in the font file.\r\n<ul>\r\n<li>\"A028\" (this looks a lot
    like Albertus but I think it's slightly different... does anyone know for sure?)\r\n<li>\"A030\"
    and \"NimbusSanL\" both appear to be Helvetica clones, which seems oddly redundant.
    \ Is there a distinction that I'm missing?\r\n<li>\"AntiqueOlive\" (Antique Olive,
    obviously)\r\n<li>\"ArtLinePrinter\" (actually, this seems to be an original Aladdin
    Software creation, not URW's work at all)\r\n<li>\"CenturySchL\" (Century Schoolbook
    implementation)\r\n<li>\"ClarendonURW\" (Clarendon, only Bold Condensed weight
    included)\r\n<li>\"Coronet\" (Coronet)\r\n<li>\"Dingbats\" (Wingdings clone)\r\n<li>\"GaramondNo8\"
    (this is a rather nice Garamond, but I'm not sure if it's an original URW interpretation
    or not.  At least, I can't perfectly match it up to one of the well-known Garamond
    designs that I've looked at.)\r\n<li>\"LetterGothic\" (Letter Gothic)\r\n<li>\"Mauritius\"
    (Mauritius)\r\n<li>\"NimbusMonL\" and \"NimbusMono\" are both included (both Courier
    clones).  Again, what's the distinction?\r\n<li>\"NimbusRomanNo4\", \"NimbusRomanNo9\",
    and \"NimbusRomNo9L\" (<i>three</i> apparent Times New Roman clones.  In fact,
    there are subtle differences between the three, but does anyone know what the
    justification for each one is?)\r\n<li>\"NimbusSanLCond\" (clone Helvetica Condensed
    or Helvetica Narrow... not totally certain which)\r\n<li>\"StandardSymL\" (Symbol)\r\n<li>\"U001\"
    (seems to be a Univers clone)\r\n<li>\"U001Con\" (similarly, a Univers Condensed
    clone)\r\n<li>\"URWBookmanL\" (Bookman)\r\n<li>\"URWChanceryL\" (Zapf Chancery)\r\n<li>\"URWClassico\"
    (Optima)\r\n<li>\"URWGothicL\" (Avant Garde Gothic)\r\n<li>\"URWPalladioL\" (Palatino)\r\n</ul>"
  created: '2012-11-29 03:18:50'
- author:
    name: altsan
    picture: 124930
  body: "<blockquote>\"NimbusRomanNo4\", \"NimbusRomanNo9\", and \"NimbusRomNo9L\"
    (three apparent Times New Roman clones. In fact, there are subtle differences
    between the three, but does anyone know what the justification for each one is?)
    </blockquote>\r\n\r\nSorry to reply to myself, but on further inspection, I should
    amend this.  All three Nimbus Roman types appear to be based on Linotype Times
    Roman.  Some interesting differences between the three, however: \r\n<ul>\r\n<li>No9
    L is almost imperceptibly lighter than the other two but in different ways: No9
    seems to have fractionally darker vertical strokes, whereas No4 has fractionally
    darker horizontals.\r\n<li>No9 has italicized Greek characters, whereas both No9L
    and No4 have regular ones.  (No9L also supports only those Greek characters appearing
    in the Latin-1 charset, whereas the other two have more.)\r\n<li>I believe No4
    has a very (<em>very</em>) slightly lower caps height than the other two.\r\n<ul>\r\n\r\nAs
    far as \"A030\" and \"Nimbus Sans L\", the latter supports Cyrillic while the
    former does not.  They otherwise seem largely identical, although I haven't done
    a rigorous inspection.\r\n\r\nI apologize if all this seems pedantic but I haven't
    been able to find much other information on the lineage of these various faces
    (esp. Nimbus Roman).  Wikipedia isn't very informative, and Google mostly seems
    to have download links rather than background."

  created: '2012-11-30 02:23:20'
- author:
    name: PabloImpallari
    picture: 119114
  body: "Renaissance Man:\r\nWe have used GPOS based kerning, instead of the old Kerning
    table. \r\nSo the 'kern' table is no longer present.\r\nAll the kerning pairs
    are contained only into the 'kern' OT feature. \r\nThis method is better for web-fonts
    since you get a smaller file size, and the font will load faster on the web.\r\n\r\nI've
    updated the description now, to make it clear and avoid confusions.\r\n"
  created: '2012-11-30 04:53:12'
- author:
    name: Michel Boyer
    picture: 112585
  body: "You can easily list the GPOS/GSUB features contained in a font with the line
    command <a href=\"http://www.lcdf.org/type/otfinfo.1.html\"><code>otfinfo</code></a><code>
    -f</code> (<code>otfinfo</code> comes with <a href=\"http://www.tug.org/mactex/\">MacTeX</a>
    and, I presume, with texlive for the PC); here are just three options for <code>otfinfo</code>
    (there are many more):\r\n<PRE>\r\nUsage: otfinfo [-sfzpg | OPTIONS] [OTFFILES...]\r\nQuery
    options:\r\n  -s, --scripts                Report font\u2019s supported scripts.\r\n
    \ -f, --features               Report font\u2019s GSUB/GPOS features.\r\n  -z,
    --optical-size           Report font\u2019s optical size information.\r\n</PRE>\r\nand
    here is the relevant trace:\r\n<PRE>\r\n517 % otfinfo -f LibreBaskerville-Regular.ttf\r\ndlig\tDiscretionary
    Ligatures\r\nfrac\tFractions\r\nkern\tKerning\r\nliga\tStandard Ligatures\r\nordn\tOrdinals\r\nsinf\tScientific
    Inferiors\r\nsups\tSuperscript\r\n518 % \r\n</PRE>\r\nMichel Boyer "

  body: "Next revision of the key:\r\n\r\n<pre>Version: 1.7\r\n\r\nLegend:\r\n\r\n
    \ Fonts:\r\n    S        Short form of the adjective Semi- (or Demi-)\r\n    U
    \       Short form of the adjective Ultra-\r\n    X        Short form of the adjective
    Extra-\r\n\r\n    Hr       Hair-thin weight\r\n    UL       Ultra-light weight\r\n
    \   XL       Extra-light weight\r\n    Lt       Light weight       (L with adjective)\r\n
    \   Th       Thin weight        (T with adjective)\r\n    Rg       Regular weight\r\n
    \   Bk       Book weight\r\n    Md       Medium weight\r\n    SB       Semi-bold
    (demi-bold) weight\r\n    Bd       Bold weight        (B with adjective)\r\n    XB
    \      Extra-bold weight\r\n    UB       Ultra-bold weight\r\n    Hv       Heavy
    weight\r\n    Bl       Black weight\r\n\r\n    I        Italic style\r\n    O
    \       Oblique style\r\n\r\n    Comp     Compressed width\r\n    Cond     Condensed
    width\r\n    SCond    Semi-Condensed width\r\n    Narr     Narrow width\r\n    Norm
    \    Normal width\r\n    SExpd    Semi-Expanded width\r\n    Expd     Expanded
    width\r\n\r\n  Format:\r\n    ADF      Apple Distortable Font (TTF with some multi-master
    features)\r\n    EOT      Embedded OpenType (Microsoft's web font format)\r\n
    \   FON      Windows bitmap font\r\n    MF       D. Knuth's Metafont\r\n    T1
    \      PostScript Type 1 (ATM font), seen in Mac, Unix and Windows flavours\r\n
    \   T1MM     PostScript Type 1 Multi-Master font\r\n    OTF      CFF-flavoured
    OpenType\r\n    SVG      Scalable Vector Graphics (useable as a font format as
    well)\r\n    TTC      TrueType Collection (a TTF library format)\r\n    TTF      TrueType
    or TT-flavoured OpenType\r\n    WOFF     Web Open Font Format (web-only font format)\r\n
    \   IK       Work file format used by Ikarus and DTL\r\n    SFD      Work file
    format used by FontForge\r\n    SFDir    Work directory format used by FontForge\r\n
    \   UFO      Work directory format used by RoboFont and others\r\n    VFB      Work
    file format used by Fontlab\r\n\r\n  Licensing:\r\n    AFPL     The Aladdin Free
    Public License (Aladdin GhostScript)\r\n    Apache   The Apache License\r\n    BSDL
    \    The BSD License, any version\r\n    CCPL     The Creative Commons Public
    License\r\n    DSL      The Free Software Foundation's Design Science License\r\n
    \   GPL      The Free Software Foundation's General Public License\r\n    GPL2+
    \   Ditto, version 2 or later\r\n    GPLfx    Ditto, with 'font exception' that
    excludes licence inheritance via embedding\r\n    GUST     The Polish TeX Users
    Group licence; Polish LPPL\r\n    LPPL     The LaTeX Project Public License\r\n
    \   MITL     The MIT License\r\n    OFL      SIL's Open Font License\r\n    UFL
    \     The Ubuntu Fint Licence\r\n    Utopia   Adobe's and TUG's collective Utopia
    licensing\r\n    Vera     Bitstream's licence for the typeface Bitstream Vera\r\n\r\nCodings:\r\n
    \   Wiegel: P. Wiegel's old code for German special glyphs\r\n    UNZ1: Subset
    of MUFI, details PUA codes for German special glyphs\r\n\r\nVersion:\r\n  The
    font/face version given is that of the Regular weight or closest equivalent.\r\n\r\nPeculiarities:\r\n
    \ 1. In ADF faces, AE, Ae, ae, OE, Oe and oe are ligated globally via 'rlig'.\r\n
    \ 2. IM Fell OTF and TTF may both be installed at the same time due to slight
    naming differences.\r\n  3. Displayed licensing does not always match between
    OTF and TTF versions of GFS faces.\r\n  4. Many GFS faces lack Latin characters.</pre>\r\n\r\nFirst

    on Windows T1 fonts\r\n    EOT      Embedded OpenType (Microsoft's web font format)\r\n

    \      PostScript Type 1 (ATM font), seen in Mac, Unix and Windows flavours\r\n

    OpenType\r\n    SVG      Scalable Vector Graphics (useable as a font format as

    or TT-flavoured OpenType\r\n    WOFF     Web Open Font Format (web-only font format)\r\n

    \   Wiegel: P. Wiegel's old 8-bit code for German special glyphs\r\n    UNZ1:
    \  Subset of MUFI, details Unicode PUA codes for German special glyphs\r\n\r\nVersion:\r\n
    \   The font/face version given is that of the Regular weight or closest equivalent.\r\n\r\nPeculiarities:\r\n
    1. In many ADF faces, AE, Ae, ae, OE, Oe and oe are ligated globally via 'rlig'.\r\n
    2. IM Fell OTF and TTF may both be installed at the same time due to slight naming
    differences.\r\n 3. Displayed licensing does not always match between OTF and
    TTF versions of GFS faces.\r\n 4. Many GFS faces lack Latin characters.\r\n\r\nReamining
    random bits of information:\r\n\r\n    MUFI: Medieval Unicode Font Initiative
    (http://www.mufi.info/)\r\n    UNZ1: Unicode-gerechte Norm f\xFCr Zusatzzeichen,
    Ausgabe 1 (http://www.bfds.de/)</pre>\r\n\r\nNext iteration of the Misc. list:\r\n\r\n<pre>Version:

  created: '2013-01-27 15:54:40'
- author:
    name: charles ellertson
  body: Aller and Ubuntu are different type families, and most importantly, have different
    licenses. As I read it, the free version of Aller is limited to 25 users, where
    25 visitors to a website counts as "25 users."
  created: '2013-01-28 02:12:08'
- author:
    name: gargoyle
    picture: 110090
  body: "Regarding Aller as a webfont, Dalton Maag considers 1 desktop user to be
    the equivalent of 1,000 unique website visitors per day, with some wiggle room
    for traffic spikes.\r\nhttp://www.daltonmaag.com/about/web_embedding_faq.html\r\n\r\nBut,
    more to the point of this thread, Aller is not an open source font; it's covered
    by [[http://www.daltonmaag.com/eula|Dalton Maag's standard EULA]] (supplemented
    with the [[http://www.daltonmaag.com/about/aller.html|Aller Free License]]), which
    does not allow modification. The Ubuntu font is covered by [[http://font.ubuntu.com/ufl/|its
    own open source license]] which is based on the SIL OFL."
  created: '2013-01-28 04:02:49'
- author:
    name: charles ellertson
  body: "Guess that's right, though confusing to me. From http://www.fontsquirrel.com/license/Aller
    (Emphasis added)\r\n\r\n<cite>1 You, the Licensee, are granted the right by Dalton
    Maag to Use the Aller Font Family, Standard Edition under the terms set out in
    the EULA. This free licence has a Licensed Unit size of 25 Users.</cite>\r\n\r\n<cite>2
    Use by more than 25 Users, <strong>or equivalent Website Visitors,</strong> is
    a breach of this Free Licence Agreement, and instead requires a commercial licence.</cite>\r\n\r\n<cite>Fontsquirrel
    Note: 1000 website visitors is the equivalent of 1 user - http://www.daltonmaag.com/about/web_embedding_faq.html</cite>\r\n\r\n"

    and [[http://www.google.com/search?hl=&q=site%3Atypophile.com+carolus|comes up
    periodically on the Type ID board]] (most recent thread [[http://typophile.com/node/98434|here]]).
    The only reason given by [[http://www.softerviews.org/Fonts.html| the AIM fonts
    website]]: \"Carita and Hattha were withdrawn as they will no longer be updated
    to match my other fonts.\" They also request others not to host or redistribute
    their fonts, which seems contradictory to the GNU license under which they're
    released."

- author:
    name: altsan
    picture: 124930
  body: "@gargoyle / @Chris : There are two sets of URW++ open source fonts.  The
    Ghostscript fonts are available under either GPL or AFPL; the GhostPCL fonts are
    AFPL only, which is technically open source but not copyleft (as it restricts
    commercial use).\r\n\r\nA while ago I put some PDF samples of the two URW++ sets
    together.  Might as well link them here.\r\nGPL Ghostscript fonts: http://www.altsan.org/fontinfo/urw_ps.pdf\r\nAFPL
    GhostPCL fonts: http://www.altsan.org/fontinfo/urw_pcl.pdf"

- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "<blockquote>it's open to forking</blockquote>\r\n\r\nThere are at least two
    reasons not to be so cavalier:\r\n1) The \"designer\" (using the term loosely
    - see #2) apparently doesn't want you to.\r\n2) It's based on a design that's
    not necessarily an orphan: http://typophile.com/node/98434\r\n\r\nBTW, it's not
    really \"your font\" unless you made it yourself. And even then you owe the people
    who came before you.\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"

  created: '2013-04-16 23:04:21'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "I doubt that anyone is looking to this thread for a definition of what open
    source is and is not in the larger world (apologies if you are). At best this
    serves as a useful discussion of the limiting factors of applying a philosophical
    model that was never conceived of to address the needs of type designers and font
    users. The OFL was conceived of to try and address the needs of designers, developers
    and users that wanted an open model which prevented restrictions, removed some
    of the ambiguity inherent in applying established licensing models (to something
    for which they were not intended) and promoted the underlying philosophy, without
    worrying about commercial considerations.\r\n\r\nPerhaps this could lead to another
    custom license, following the Apache model but focusing on type/fonts? The Typophile
    Apache Licence (TAL), anyone? : )"
  created: '2013-04-17 12:59:45'
- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "I can dig that.\r\nSo exactly how does the existing Apache model not serve
    type well?\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"
  created: '2013-04-17 14:21:48'
- author:
    name: Karl Stange
    picture: 118040
  body: "<blockquote>So exactly how does the existing Apache model not serve type
    well?</blockquote>\r\n\r\nIf you are using it and happy with it then I suppose
    that it does, but as it is not explicitly designed around type and the ways that
    type can be used then it is possible that it will fall short in some respect.
    I suppose that interpretation of section 3. 'Grant of Patent License' is confusing
    enough that I would not be comfortable taking advantage of it in a commercial
    capacity unless I was clear on the lineage of the typeface and the contributions
    made to it, basically, I would want to know where it had been."

- author:
    name: vernon adams
    picture: 121155
  body: "@Karl Stang\r\n\r\n<blockquote>The OFL was conceived of to try and address
    the needs of designers, developers and users that wanted an open model which prevented
    restrictions, removed some of the ambiguity inherent in applying established licensing
    models (to something for which they were not intended) and promoted the underlying
    philosophy, without worrying about commercial considerations.\r\n\r\nPerhaps this
    could lead to another custom license, following the Apache model but focusing
    on type/fonts? The Typophile Apache Licence (TAL), anyone? : )</blockquote>\r\n\r\nI
    wonder if the OFL is a little too slightly a product of it's specific origins.
    It's need was to promote the spread of freely useable fonts. That need was tied
    to SIL's work to promote the spread of freely available [religious] texts. There
    was naturally less of a need to protect the designs themselves. I would like to
    see an OFL (or OFL like license) that is stronger in it's protection of free fonts
    from the potential dangers of other designers or foundries taking free fonts (or
    parts of them) and privatising them.\r\n"
  created: '2013-05-19 17:42:00'
- author:
    name: hrant
    picture: 110403
  body: "Since there now are virtually no barriers (well, besides talent and time
    :-) to creating good free fonts, I believe blocking people from making money from
    fonts actually reduces social justice.\r\n\r\nFor example somebody can make an
    Armenian extension to Roboto Slab and give it away, while I can make one and sell
    it, and people can freely choose the free one or the good one. :-)  No hard feelings,
    and everybody is happy. If I weren't allowed to make money from it, Armenian culture
    just lost a good font.\r\n\r\nVirtually nobody capable of contributing something
    of quality to society says: \"I'd like to be compensated doing this, but I'm not
    allowed... but let me just do it anyway.\" They either would do it for no compensation
    to begin with (so the option of making money doing it is no impediment) or would
    spend the time doing something else!\r\n\r\nhhp\r\n"
  created: '2013-05-19 18:27:01'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Vernon: <em>I would like to see an OFL (or OFL like license) that is stronger
    in it's protection of free fonts from the potential dangers of other designers
    or foundries taking free fonts (or parts of them) and privatising them.</em>\r\n\r\nThe
    first of the Permissions and Conditions terms of the OFL license is this:\r\n\r\n<blockquote>1)
    Neither the Font Software nor any of its individual components, in Original or
    Modified Versions, may be sold by itself.</blockquote>\r\n\r\nAn OFL font may
    be bundled with a commercial product, but cannot be sold as a product in itself.
    I'm not sure what else you mean by 'privatising' free fonts. What is your concern?\r\n\r\n[When
    Ralph Hancock and I decided to make our Biblical Hebrew layout model open source,
    we deliberately avoided the OFL because it prevents utilisation in commercial
    fonts. We opted for the more liberal MIT license instead.]"
  created: '2013-05-19 18:40:57'

  created: '2013-05-19 18:52:41'
- author:
    name: vernon adams
    picture: 121155
  body: "Hrant\r\n<blockquote>somebody can make an Armenian extension to Roboto Slab
    and give it away, while I can make one and sell it</blockquote>\r\n\r\nThis is
    where a license can protects a font's freedom;\r\nUnder the terms of the OFL,
    you specifically cannot sell an OFL'd font (or a component of it) or sell a font
    derived from an OFL'd font.\r\nI don't think the Apache license (as i understand
    it, so i could be wrong!) specifically prohibits selling an Apache'd font , but
    in practise it would probably be a non starter (if i am correct) because ;\r\nIf
    you were to make an Armenian extension of Roboto Slab, you would have to distribute
    it under the same Apache license as Roboto Slab (i.e make it 'free'), otherwise
    you would be breaching the font's license, naughty. So, the world would have Hrant's
    Roboto Slab... complete with the Apache license. So... whilst you may find a few
    people who will pay you to use your version of Roboto Slab, most will probably
    simply use it for free, under the terms of it's Apache license.\r\nAlso, of course,
    under the terms of Roboto Slab's license, your Armenian extenstion would possibly
    got folded back into the main Roboto Slab branch.\r\n\r\nAre you saying that preventing
    other designers from 'privatising' derivatives of free fonts is reducing social
    justice?  I would 100% agree with you, under certain circumstances, aka, if anyone
    could privatise a free font, and if anyone could free a proprietary font :) Cuts
    both way surely? The intellectual property contained in a free font is not of
    an inferior kind compared to the intellectual property contained in a proprietary
    font. Or do you think it is?\r\n"
  created: '2013-05-19 18:58:26'
- author:
    name: vernon adams
    picture: 121155
  body: "<blockquote>I'm not sure what else you mean by 'privatising' free fonts.
    What is your concern?</blockquote>\r\nMy concern is that 'free' licenses are generally
    seen as being about 'giving stuff away' and less about 'protecting the freedom'
    of the designer's output. I accept that (like with proprietary fonts) you can
    never reduce unlicensed use to 0%, but i think free software can too easilly have
    it's licensing terms disregarded, either by accident or design. Free software
    can be seen as a soft target for unlicensed use. The preamble of the OFL, for
    example, could make it a little clearer imo. No harm in that i would think; maybe
    more people would understand what they can and cannot do.\r\n\r\n<blockquote>I'm
    guessing Vernon would like a license that prohibits commercial bundling.</blockquote>\r\nNo,
    i love the commercial bundling of free fonts :)"
